в—Ҹ I've traced the codebase carefully. Here are the most likely sources of RAM
  growth on the 96-core run, separated by mechanism (true leak vs. GC pressure):

  True unbounded growth (memory keeps climbing forever)

  These accumulate references that GC cannot reclaim:

  1. model.global_iter_dict is never cleared вҖ”
  src/global_plasticity_loop/utils/LoopInformation.jl:74
  Every Picard iteration of every timestep pushes a new Vector{Any} into this
  dict (~5 entries/timestep). It's saved to YAML each plasticity loop but never 
  reset. After 1000s of timesteps Г— ~5 iters = tens of thousands of Vector{Any}
  entries. Each entry holds 9 elements including a String, so this is
  heap-fragmenting too.
  Fix: clear/empty between timesteps after save_iteration_info_to_file, or just
  save+truncate each time.

  2. XDMF chunk vectors are never cleared вҖ” 4 instances:
  - src/model_manager/xdmf/markers/core/TimeSteps.jl:19 вҖ”
  push!(markers_xdmf.xdmf_chunks, string)
  - src/model_manager/xdmf/fields/core/TimeSteps.jl:20
  - src/model_manager/xdmf/topography/core/TimeSteps.jl:19
  - src/model_manager/xdmf/velocity/core/TimeSteps.jl (same pattern)

  Every output step pushes a new XDMF string (typically 1вҖ“10 KB). Vectors grow
  forever, plus the XDMF file is fully rewritten from accumulated chunks each
  save. For a long run with thousands of output steps Г— 4 collections, this can
  become tens to hundreds of MB.
  Fix: write incrementally (open/append/close) instead of holding all chunks in
  memory; or write the closing tag and rewrite the file each output step without
   keeping the in-memory vector.

  Heavy GC pressure (looks like growth on 96 cores)

  Julia's GC scales poorly with thread count. On 96 threads, allocation rate
  dominates GC throughput, so the resident set grows even though references are
  dropped. These are the highest-volume allocators:

  3. clean_non_zero_arrays! slices the build buffers, allocating fresh huge 
  arrays each Picard iteration вҖ” src/utils/BuildSysTools.jl:113-116
  return SystemVectors(
      sv.Lii[1:nnz], sv.Ljj[1:nnz],
      sv.Li[1:nnz], sv.Lj[1:nnz], sv.Lv[1:nnz]
  )
  Despite the buffers in system_vectors being preallocated, this slice copies
  them. For a typical Stokes problem nnz is tens of millions; that's ~5 Г— nnz Г—
  8 B вүҲ hundreds of MB allocated and discarded per Picard iteration (often
  5вҖ“10/timestep). This is probably the single biggest GC pressure source after
  the recent melting/compaction fixes.
  Fix: track nnz and pass view(sv.Li, 1:nnz) into sparse(...), or build the
  sparse matrix in-place using SparseMatrixCSC constructors that accept the full
   buffers + a length.

  4. Ls = sparse(Li, Lj, Lv, N, N) вҖ” fresh sparse matrix per Picard iteration вҖ”
  src/stokes_continuity_solver/solver/system_solver/SystemSolver.jl:33 and
  src/heat_solver/solver/SystemSolver.jl:40
  The CSC constructor allocates colptr (N+1 Int64), rowval (nnz Int64), nzval
  (nnz Float64), plus internal sort buffers. Allocated, used, dropped, repeat.
  With Stokes calling this 5вҖ“10Г—/timestep and heat once, this dwarfs almost
  everything else for large grids.
  Fix: build into a persistent SparseMatrixCSC with stable sparsity pattern
  using sparse! from SparseArrays (Julia 1.9+) or maintain your own CSC buffers.

  5. update_*_on_basic_grid! family does arr.array = copy(...) вҖ” src/global_plas
  ticity_loop/plasticity_loop/plasticity_loop_nodes/PlasticityLoopNodes.jl:111, 
  112, 119, 126, 134, 143, 144
  Every Picard iteration reassigns
  model.stokes_continuity.arrays.viscosity.etas1.array = copy(etavp_new) (and 5
  similar lines). Each is a grid-sized matrix copy. Replace with
  copyto!(modelвҖҰ.array, etavp_new) вҖ” keeps the original buffer.

  6. update_nodes_for_plastic_yielding allocates 2 grid-sized matrices per 
  Picard iter вҖ” src/rheology/plastic_failure/PlasticFailureNodes.jl:72-73
  plastic_yield_new = zeros(ynum, xnum)
  plastic_strain_rate = zeros(ynum, xnum)
  Returned to caller and then copied into model arrays at #5 above вҖ” so they get
   allocated, used, and immediately discarded. Preallocate as model buffer.

  7. MUMPS RHS copy each solve вҖ”
  src/stokes_continuity_solver/solver/system_solver/SystemSolver.jl:36 and
  src/heat_solver/solver/SystemSolver.jl:44
  R = copy(model.stokes_continuity.arrays.rhs.RHS.array)
  Big Vector{Float64} (size N), allocated per iteration. If MUMPS doesn't mutate
   it, drop the copy and pass the array directly. If it does, preallocate a
  persistent buffer and use copyto!.

  8. Material-ID list dicts allocated per call вҖ”
  src/markers/marker_materials/utils/MaterialGroupIDs.jl:216 and
  src/markers/marker_materials/get_ids/GetMaterialIDs.jl:226
  get_material_id_lists() builds a fresh Dict{String, Vector{Int16}} with ~10
  vectors via collect + append! every call. Called from many places per timestep
   (rheology, melt, serpentinization, surface processes, hydrothermal, etc.).
  Small individually, but called dozens of times per timestep Г— thousands of
  steps = significant GC churn.
  Fix: cache the result as a model field; only invalidate if the materials list
  changes (likely never during run).

  9. BilinearAverage copies of stress/heat arrays passed as args вҖ”
  src/interpolation/.../BilinearAverage.jl:240, 246, 261
  copy(stress_change.dsxyn.array) etc. used as backups during markerвҶ’grid
  interpolation each timestep. Replace with persistent backup arrays (copyto!).

  10. ViscosityRestriction.jl:96-122 вҖ” 6 viscosity copies per multigrid cycle
  If multigrid is being used (V-cycles per Stokes solve), this is heavy. Use
  copyto! into preallocated buffers.

  11. extrusion_volumes_o = copy(extrusion_volumes) вҖ”
  src/melt_model/.../Drainage.jl:244
  Per-timestep copy in melt extraction. Use a persistent buffer.

  12. get_mantle_melting_ids / get_mantle_emplacement_ids allocate per call вҖ”
  src/melt_model/.../Extraction.jl:577, 633 and
  src/melt_model/melt_rheology/MeltRheology.jl:105
  Called multiple times per timestep, including inside threaded loops. Cache
  once per timestep.

  Lower priority / one-time

  - src/staggered_grid/utils/Spacing.jl:141-156 вҖ” zeros(...) allocations during
  regrid (only on regrid steps; bounded).
  - src/model_manager/xdmf/XdmfTimeStepsManager.jl:64-69 вҖ” coordinate-array
  copies on output steps only.
  - Plot manager copies in PlotVectorArraysManager.jl, PlotGridArraysManager.jl
  вҖ” only on plot output.

  Recommended next steps

  1. Fix #1 and #2 first вҖ” these are true leaks; everything else is GC-related.
  2. Run with --heap-size-hint=80G (or similar) to give Julia's GC a target вҖ” it
   often delays collection until heap pressure forces it.
  3. The big GC-pressure items in priority order: #3 (slice copies in
  clean_non_zero_arrays!) вҶ’ #4 (sparse matrix construction) вҶ’ #5/#6 (plasticity
  loop copies) вҶ’ #7 (MUMPS RHS) вҶ’ #8 (material id dicts).
  4. Add a periodic GC.gc() call at the end of each timestep loop on the 96-core
   run to confirm: if memory drops back after a forced GC, the issue is GC not
  keeping up (items #3вҖ“#12). If memory stays elevated, items #1вҖ“#2 are confirmed
   leaks.

  Want me to draft fixes for #1 and #2, or start with the high-volume sparse
  matrix path (#3)?

вқҜ But this issues gets worse on the machines with more threads                  

в—Ҹ That's a strong signal that the problem is GC contention, not leaks вҖ” items #1
   and #2 (unbounded growth) wouldn't scale with thread count. Let me search for
   allocations inside threaded hot paths specifically.

  Searched for 9 patterns, read 6 files, listed 1 directory (ctrl+o to expand)

в—Ҹ Here's the picture given that thread count amplifies the problem.

  Why scaling makes this worse

  Julia's GC is partially stop-the-world and doesn't scale linearly with
  threads. With 96 threads each touching model.markers.arrays.* arrays of
  millions of elements via Threads.@threads, every per-Picard / per-timestep
  heap allocation is now competing with 96Г— the rate of mark/sweep work. Two
  things happen:

  1. GC defers collection вҖ” Julia's heuristic prefers heap growth over pausing
  96 threads. Resident set climbs even with no leaks.
  2. Heap fragmentation вҖ” repeated alloc/free of huge sparse matrices and
  grid-sized arrays creates holes the allocator can't reuse efficiently.

  So allocations that look harmless single-threaded (allocate, GC reclaims)
  behave like leaks at high thread count.

  What actually scales with threads here

  Ranked by per-timestep allocation volume Г— likelihood of being on the hot
  path:

  1. clean_non_zero_arrays! slicing build buffers вҖ”
  src/utils/BuildSysTools.jl:113-116
  Five sv.Lii[1:nnz]-style slice copies per Picard iteration. For a Stokes
  problem, nnz is tens of millions вҶ’ ~5 Г— nnz Г— 8 B вүҲ several hundred MB
  allocated and discarded per Picard iteration (5вҖ“10Г—/timestep). Single biggest
  allocation source. Replace with view(...) passed directly into sparse(...).

  2. Ls = sparse(Li, Lj, Lv, N, N) вҖ”
  src/stokes_continuity_solver/.../SystemSolver.jl:33 and
  src/heat_solver/.../SystemSolver.jl:40
  Constructs a fresh SparseMatrixCSC per Picard iteration. Allocates colptr
  (N+1), rowval (nnz), nzval (nnz) plus internal sort buffers. Same order of
  magnitude as #1. Keep a persistent matrix and use sparse! (Julia вүҘ1.9) once
  the sparsity pattern is stable.

  3. arr.array = copy(...) in plasticity loop вҖ” src/global_plasticity_loop/plast
  icity_loop/plasticity_loop_nodes/PlasticityLoopNodes.jl:111, 112, 119, 126, 
  134, 143, 144
  6 grid-sized matrix copies per Picard iter (etas1, plastic_yield,
  plastic_strain_rate). Replace each arr.array = copy(x) with copyto!(arr.array,
   x) вҖ” keeps the existing buffer.

  4. update_nodes_for_plastic_yielding returns 2 grid-sized zeros вҖ”
  src/rheology/plastic_failure/PlasticFailureNodes.jl:72-73
  Allocated, returned, then immediately copied into model arrays at #3.
  Preallocate as a model field; pass in for in-place fill.

  5. MUMPS RHS copy per solve вҖ”
  src/stokes_continuity_solver/.../SystemSolver.jl:36 and
  src/heat_solver/.../SystemSolver.jl:44
  R = copy(model.вҖҰRHS.array) вҖ” Vector{Float64} of size N, allocated per solve.
  Use a persistent buffer with copyto!.

  6. BilinearAverage copies inside arg list вҖ”
  src/interpolation/utils/BilinearAverage.jl:240, 246, 261
  copy(stress_change.dsxyn.array) etc. used as backups during markerвҶ’grid
  interpolation each timestep. Three grid-sized copies/step. Use persistent
  backup arrays.

  7. ViscosityRestriction.jl:96-122 вҖ” 6 viscosity copies per multigrid V-cycle
  (only matters if multigrid is on the active path; if MUMPS is being used, skip
   this).

  8. get_material_id_lists builds a fresh dict per call вҖ”
  src/markers/marker_materials/utils/MaterialGroupIDs.jl:216
  A Dict{String, Vector{Int16}} with ~10 vectors via collect + append!. Called
  from rheology, melt, serpentinization, surface processes, hydrothermal вҖ”
  easily 20+ calls/timestep, each from threaded code paths. Cache once on the
  model; invalidate only if material set changes (probably never during run).

  9. get_mantle_melting_ids / get_mantle_emplacement_ids вҖ”
  src/melt_model/extraction/Extraction.jl:577, 633 and
  src/melt_model/.../MeltRheology.jl:105
  Allocates fresh Vector{Int16} each call, called inside or right before
  Threads.@threads loops. Cache per timestep.

  Operational knobs that help on 96 threads

  These don't fix the allocations but reduce the symptom:

  - --heap-size-hint=NN (Julia вүҘ1.10) вҖ” tell Julia your memory budget so GC runs
   more aggressively before resident set explodes. With ~doubled RAM, set this
  to roughly the post-preallocation working set + 30%. Often reduces resident
  memory dramatically without code changes.
  - --gcthreads=N (Julia вүҘ1.10) вҖ” by default Julia uses ~half the threads for GC
   marking. With 96 worker threads, default may be too few. Try --gcthreads=24,1
   (24 mark + 1 concurrent sweep).
  - GC.gc() once per timestep at the end of run_loop! in
  src/time_loop/TimeLoop.jl:149-164 вҖ” diagnostic. If forcing GC each step keeps
  memory flat, the issue is purely GC cadence and items #1вҖ“#9 are just feeding
  it. If it still climbs, you have a true reference leak (then look back at the
  convergence dict and XDMF chunks I flagged previously).

  Suggested attack order

  1. Add --heap-size-hint and a per-timestep GC.gc() to the 96-core run вҖ”
  confirms it's GC pacing, takes 5 minutes.
  2. Fix #1 (view instead of slicing). This alone may halve the GC pressure.
  3. Fix #2 (persistent sparse matrix) вҖ” biggest remaining win.
  4. Fix #3 + #4 together (they're the same code path).
  5. Fix #5вҖ“#9 in any order; each is a few-line change.

  Want me to draft fixes for #1 and #3/#4 first, since those are the
  highest-volume and the smallest edits?

вқҜ Give me an example of of I would use --heap-size-hint and what I should set   
  it to                                                                         

в—Ҹ Syntax

  --heap-size-hint is a Julia CLI flag (вүҘ1.10), accepting K / M / G / T suffixes
   or a percentage:

  julia --heap-size-hint=64G --threads=96 your_script.jl

  Or as a percentage of total system RAM (вүҘ1.11):

  julia --heap-size-hint=70% --threads=96 your_script.jl

  If you're using a project + driver script:

  julia --project=. --heap-size-hint=64G --threads=96 -e 'using EarthBox; вҖҰ'

  It's a soft target, not a hard cap. Julia uses it to schedule GC more
  aggressively as the heap approaches that size вҖ” which is exactly what you want
   when GC is being lazy under high allocation pressure.

  Picking the value

  Rule of thumb: ~1.3Г— your post-preallocation working set, but well below total
   system RAM.

  Two ways to estimate:

  1. From a short run. Start the simulation, let preallocation finish, take the
  resident set after the first few timesteps stabilize. That's your baseline B.
  Set --heap-size-hint=1.3*B. With your "doubled" observation, if peak is
  currently 2Г— preallocation, you want the hint to land around the preallocation
   level + 30%, forcing GC before the heap doubles.

  2. From system RAM. Leave headroom for OS, MUMPS native buffers, MPI, file
  caches:
  - 256 GB box вҶ’ --heap-size-hint=180G
  - 512 GB box вҶ’ --heap-size-hint=380G
  - 1 TB box вҶ’ --heap-size-hint=750G

  Generally 60вҖ“75% of physical RAM is the safe band for a single Julia process.

  Concrete example for your case

  If you don't yet know your preallocation footprint, do this on the 96-core
  machine:

  # First run вҖ” measure the baseline
  julia --project=. --threads=96 -e 'using EarthBox; вҖҰ' 2>&1 | tee run.log
  # Look for the @timeit_memit "MB :" lines after preallocation finishes.
  # Say the steady-state shows ~50 GB.

  # Second run вҖ” pin GC to that target + 30%
  julia --project=. --heap-size-hint=65G --threads=96 -e 'using EarthBox; вҖҰ'

  If the hint is too low you'll see heavy GC time in the timing macros (CPU time
   per step jumps). If it's too high, you're back to the original problem.
  Bisect between baseline and physical-RAM-cap.

  Pair it with --gcthreads on 96 cores

  The default GC thread count is too small for a 96-thread allocator load. Try:

  julia --project=. \
        --threads=96 \
        --gcthreads=24,1 \
        --heap-size-hint=65G \
        -e 'using EarthBox; вҖҰ'

  --gcthreads=24,1 = 24 parallel mark threads + 1 concurrent sweep thread. 24 (=
   96/4) is a reasonable starting point; tune up if GC still dominates.

вқҜ I have a huge log file from the 96 core model run. Would it be possible for   
  you to effciently analyze this file with out spending a rediculous number of  
  my tokens?                                                                    

в—Ҹ Yes вҖ” the trick is to never read the file into my context. I run targeted
  shell commands that emit only summary lines.

  Plan

  1. Size the file (wc -l, byte size) so we know what we're dealing with.
  2. Extract just the memory readings. Your @timeit_memit macro prints MB : 
  XX.XX after every timed section (src/utils/PrintFuncs.jl:88). One grep | awk
  collapses millions of lines into a numeric trace of resident memory over time.
  3. Compute the growth trend. A small awk script that takes
  min/max/first/last/slope of the MB column. Output is a few lines.
  4. Check for the specific suspects вҖ” count occurrences of "Stokes Iteration",
  "Picard", "Finished executing pre-solver steps", etc., and report memory at
  each.
  5. Optional: bin per timestep. If the log has "Completed time step N" markers
  (TimeLoop.jl:122 prints those), bucket the memory readings between those
  markers and emit one max-MB-per-timestep line. That gives a clean
  monotonic-or-not trace I can scan without reading every line.

  Total tokens consumed: just the summary outputs (handful of numbers + a few
  hundred lines max), not the log itself.

  What I need from you

  Just the path to the log file (and confirm it's plain text, not gzipped вҖ” if
  gzipped, no problem, I'll pipe through zcat).

  Optionally, tell me:
  - Roughly how many timesteps it ran (helps me pick a sampling rate if the file
   is enormous)
  - Whether it ran to completion or OOM-killed

  Once I have the path I'll run a few targeted commands and report back the
  trend, where the growth happens within each timestep, and which sections show
  the steepest per-step deltas.

  Important caveat: these are Sys.maxrss() readings, which are historical 
  high-water marks, not current memory. Once a value is hit it never decreases.
  The fact that the trace plateaus at step 1099 means peak memory has been
  bounded for the last ~200 timesteps вҖ” actual current memory may be lower. So
  this is NOT an unbounded leak.

  в””в”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”ҙв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”ҙв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”ҙв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”ҳ

  Diagnosis

  ~90% of the 45 GB growth comes from extract_melt_in_drainage_basins! in
  src/melt_model/extraction/Extraction.jl:228. The pattern is consistent with
  transient buffers that scale with the partial-melt marker count, which itself
  grows over the run as more mantle becomes melt-active. Each extraction event
  temporarily allocates more than the previous high-water mark, so maxrss
  ratchets up. Once the partial-melt zone reaches its quasi-steady extent
  (around step 1100), the peak stops growing.

  Specific suspects inside the function (worth tightening):

  - Line 244-245: mantle_melting_mat_ids = get_mantle_melting_ids(model) and
  mantle_emplacement_mat_ids = get_mantle_emplacement_ids(model) вҖ” fresh
  Vector{Int16} per call (and called inside the per-step extraction).
  - Line 264-267: MeltVolumetrics.calculate_melt_volume(...) over the full
  domain.
  - Line 287-288: same function called inside the per-basin loop.
  - Line 247: Extractable.update_extractable_meltfrac!(...) вҖ” likely threaded
  over markers.
  - Line 251: Extracted.update_extracted_meltfrac!(...) вҖ” same.
  - Line 333: MagmaBody.extract_partial_melt_and_make_magma_body(...) вҖ” even
  with the recent buffer fixes, residual allocations may still scale with
  nmarkers_partial_melt.

  The XDMF output spikes (~1.1 GB each) are independent вҖ” that's the
  marker-array-to-disk flow you've already been working on in TimeStep.jl.

  Bottom line

  - This is GC behavior + growing transient working set, not a leak. The plateau
   confirms it.
  - The simulation does in fact reach a stable peak; the worry of "doubling
  indefinitely" is not borne out by the log.
  - --heap-size-hint=80G plus --gcthreads=24,1 would likely cap the resident set
   well below 95 GB by forcing earlier GC during melt-extraction transients.
  - If you want to reduce the transient peak rather than just GC it faster,
  focus next on MeltVolumetrics.calculate_melt_volume and
  update_extractable_meltfrac! / update_extracted_meltfrac! вҖ” those scale with
  the partial-melt marker count and are the logical next targets after the
  MagmaBody buffer work you already did.

  Token cost of the analysis: roughly 200 lines of awk output, none of the 1.1M
  log lines. Want me to dig into MeltVolumetrics.calculate_melt_volume to
  confirm the per-call allocation hypothesis?

в—Ҹ That's a crucial piece of context вҖ” it changes the conclusion considerably.

  The 38.7 GB of growth attributed to extract_melt_from_mantle_to_magma_bodies
  is exactly the surface area your recent commits targeted:

  - f575e92 вҖ” preallocated compaction_array
  - cc277d3 вҖ” buffers in construct_layered_partially_molten_arrays!
  - 49764ed вҖ” preallocated buffer for
  calculate_marker_indices_mantle_search_domain!
  - ff7dc4e вҖ” copyto! for marker indices
  - db36c6c вҖ” eliminated copy(grid[1, :]) patterns

  These fixes target the exact functions called from
  extract_melt_in_drainage_basins! (Extraction.jl:228). So the picture is:

  - The log shows pre-fix behavior. Extraction was the dominant source of the
  maxrss climb on the 96-core run.
  - The plateau at step 1099 in the old code suggests that even unfixed, the
  transient peak was bounded once the melt zone reached quasi-steady extent вҖ”
  which is consistent with growing-but-bounded working set rather than a true
  leak.
  - Your recent commits should substantially reduce both the per-extraction 
  transient peak and its growth rate. A re-run of case4 on the 96-core machine
  with the current branch is the right next step; rerunning the same
  delta-attribution analysis on the new log will tell you whether extraction is
  still dominant.

  What I'd recommend:

  1. Deploy current main to the 96-core machine and re-run case4 вҖ” even just the
   first few hundred timesteps would be enough to see whether the per-call jumps
   in the extraction sections shrink.
  2. Add --heap-size-hint=70G --gcthreads=24,1 to that run regardless. Even with
   the allocation fixes, the GC scheduling problem on 96 threads is independent.
  3. If the new log still shows extraction-driven growth, the remaining suspects
   (MeltVolumetrics.calculate_melt_volume,
  Extractable.update_extractable_meltfrac!,
  Extracted.update_extracted_meltfrac!) are the next places to apply the same
  buffer-reuse pattern.
  4. The non-extraction items I flagged earlier (clean_non_zero_arrays! slicing,
   sparse matrix reconstruction, plasticity-loop copy()s, etc.) become more
  important relative to extraction once these fixes land вҖ” but they were always
  second-order on this run, contributing at most a few GB out ofВ 45.

  Want me to grep the analysis output for whether anything non-extraction was
  meaningful in this log, so you have a baseline of "what remains after the
  extraction fixes"?

в—Ҹ Good questions. Let me dig into both.

  Searched for 2 patterns, read 7 files (ctrl+o to expand)

в—Ҹ Did it really plateau? Low confidence.

  Three new facts change the picture:

  1. The run didn't terminate cleanly. No Total time taken to run time loop
  message, no clean shutdown вҖ” the log cuts off mid-Stokes-solver inside a
  timestep. So we have no end-state evidence; the trace just stops.
  2. The "plateau" hides a final tick. New high-water marks over time (line
  number, MB):
  line  928250 вҶ’ 95507.5  (вүҲstep 1060, last "big" jump of 334 MB)
  line 1105552 вҶ’ 95606.3  (вүҲstep 1297, +99 MB after ~250 flat steps)
  2. So the trace wasn't flat вҖ” there was a fresh +99 MB high-water mark just 3
  timesteps before the log was cut off. A 250-timestep gap of flatness followed
  by a new high is not evidence of stability; it's evidence that random
  transient peaks didn't beat the previous one for a while, then one finally
  did.
  3. The growth is decelerating, not stopped. Per-jump sizes shrank meaningfully
   toward the end (800+ MB jumps around step 900 вҶ’ ~500 MB by step 1042 вҶ’ ~99 MB
   by stepВ 1297). That's consistent with the partial-melt working set
  asymptotically approaching its steady extent, but not proof it's reached it.
  If you'd let the simulation run another 1000 steps you'd likely have seen a
  few more small new highs before genuine flatness.

  My honest read: the working set was converging but had not actually converged
  by step 1300. Whether it would settle at, say, 96 GB or drift to 100+ GB given
   another 2000 steps, the log can't tell us.

  What this tells you about the next run

  After the extraction-fix commits land, the residual hot spots will be:

  1. XDMF output is now your single biggest individual allocation event (3.3 GB
  peak). After the GC.gc(false) work in
  src/model_manager/xdmf/markers/core/TimeStep.jl it's likely smaller, but worth
   re-measuring. Each output dump materializes the marker arrays in memory
  transiently.

  2. calculating melt drainage divides (src/melt_model/drainage/) is a real 
  recurring contributor. 1.6 GB across 9 events isn't tiny, and like extraction
  it scales with active drainage extent. After the Extraction.jl fixes, this is
  the next melt-side function to look at вҖ” same buffer-reuse pattern would
  apply.

  3. sediment transport allocations may scale with shoreline activity вҖ” moderate
   but not trivial.

  4. The Stokes build / sparse matrix concerns I flagged earlier 
  (clean_non_zero_arrays!, sparse(...)) are NOT showing up as memory growth in 
  this log. The maxrss-based instrumentation can only see growth in the
  high-water mark, and once the first Stokes build sets that mark, subsequent
  rebuilds at the same nnz size never bump it. So those allocations are real GC
  pressure (which is what scales badly with thread count), but they're invisible
   to this log analysis. Whether they actually matter on the 96-core run depends
   on whether GC keeps up вҖ” which is what --heap-size-hint is for.

  5. JIT/first-call allocations (~2.5 GB total) are a fixed one-time cost and 
  won't grow with run length.

  Recommendation

  When the next 96-core run is done, re-run the same delta analysis. If
  extraction is no longer the dominant section, drainage-divides + XDMF output
  will be next. And if maxrss is still climbing at the end of that run, the
  answer is: it's the asymptote that matters, not whether the curve has
  flattened вҖ” keep --heap-size-hint set to your real budget.

вқҜ Are you taking into account the way timeitmemit works? That is if a function  
  is wrapped in this does the memory refelct the before or after RAM state of   
  the function                                                                  

в—Ҹ Yes, but let me make it explicit вҖ” and verify.

  How the macro actually works

  From src/utils/PrintFuncs.jl:55-70:

  local mem_mb = Sys.maxrss() / 1024 / 1024   # вҶҗ captured BEFORE expr
  local time1 = time()
  result = $(esc(expr))                        # вҶҗ expr runs here
  local time2 = time()
  print_performance(description_str, delta_time, mem_mb, level_val)

  The Sys.maxrss() call happens before the wrapped expression runs, then prints
  after. So a log line that says

  Finished X : MB 46259.65

  reports memory at the start of section X, not the end. The label is somewhat
  misleading вҖ” mem_mb was captured before X did anything.

  What that means for the delta analysis

  For two consecutive log lines from sections that run back-to-back:

  Finished A : MB Y_A   вҶҗ memory at start of A
  Finished B : MB Y_B   вҶҗ memory at start of B = memory at end of A

  Therefore Y_B вҲ’ Y_A = growth that occurred during section A (not B).

  In my awk script:

  if (delta > 1) {
      desc = description_from_prev_line     # вҶҗ prev line, i.e. A
      sumdelta[desc] += delta
  }

  I attributed each delta to prev_line's description, which is the section where
   the growth actually happened. So the attribution direction is correct.

  Sanity check on real entries

  Take Finished extracting melt from mantle to magma bodies from the table. The
  reading on its own line is memory at the start of the extraction call. The
  reading on the next log line (the section that runs right after extraction in
  the post-solver chain) is the memory at the end of extraction. Their
  difference correctly attributes the growth to extraction. The fact that this
  attribution is consistent with the source code structure of
  extract_melt_in_drainage_basins! (which we know allocates per-call) is
  corroborating evidence that the math is right.

  One genuine subtlety I should flag

  There's a corner case: if section A is itself a @timeit_memit block that
  contains other @timeit_memit blocks (the #-prefixed wrapper sections like # 
  Finished executing post-solver steps), the inner readings interleave with
  outer ones. In the log, the sequence around such a block looks like:

  Finished outer-section-start          : MB Y0   (start of outer)
  Finished inner-1                      : MB Y0   (still start, no work yet)
  Finished inner-2                      : MB Y1
  вҖҰ
  # Finished executing post-solver steps : MB Y_outer_end

  So the #-prefixed line shows memory at the end of all the inner sections
  (because by then the outer macro is finally printing what it captured at the
  very start, but the next section after the wrapper has its own start-reading).
   That's why in my table, the outer wrapper # Finished executing post-solver 
  steps shows ~+1 GB more cumulative growth than the inner Finished updating 
  melt extraction (41.0 vs 40.4 GB) вҖ” the outer captures growth from
  sub-sections that run after extraction (sealevel update, regrid, marker
  recycling, etc.). The accounting still balances; it's just a hierarchy.

  Bottom line

  Yes вҖ” the analysis correctly attributes growth to the section in which it
  occurred. The Sys.maxrss() semantics (monotonic, captured-before) was the
  basis for both the per-section delta table and the "this is a high-water mark,
   not current memory" caveat about the plateau.

  divides. Together they account for ~40.3 GB of the 45 GB.

  If your recent extraction commits eliminate even half of that transient peak,
  the next run's growth budget drops from ~45 GB to ~25 GB or less, and XDMF
  output (item 2) becomes the single largest individual event by a wide margin.

  What's NOT showing up that we discussed earlier

  The Stokes-build / sparse-matrix / plasticity-loop copy() allocations only
  contribute their first-call high-water mark (~0.3 GB total). They don't bump
  maxrss on subsequent calls because the working set is the same size each time
  вҖ” but they're still real GC pressure that scales with thread count, just
  invisible to this metric. That's the part --heap-size-hint and --gcthreads are
   for.

вң» SautГ©ed for 18s

вқҜ So since we made these recent commits that reduce allocations do you think    
  the amount of RAM held by the GC will reduce?                                 

в—Ҹ Short answer: yes, but with two important caveats.

  Why it should reduce

  The recent commits don't just reduce GC churn вҖ” they reduce the peak transient
   working set. Each preallocation replaces a zeros(...) / copy(...) that was
  being created and discarded with a buffer that exists once and lives forever.
  The transient peak during extraction is what drove Sys.maxrss() upward;
  eliminating those transients should lower the high-water mark of resident
  memory:

  - Lower peak. Julia's heap grows opportunistically to accommodate live objects
   + headroom. If the live-object peak during extraction drops by hundreds of MB
   per call, the heap (and therefore RSS) doesn't need to grow as far.
  - Less GC deferral. With 96 threads, the GC was deferring collection under
  high allocation pressure. Lower pressure вҶ’ GC runs more proactively вҶ’ smaller
  working set between collections.
  - Less fragmentation. Repeated allocate/free of large grid-sized arrays
  creates holes the allocator can't always reuse efficiently. Buffers reused in
  place don't fragment.

  Given that extraction was ~86% of the recurring growth in case4, and the
  commits target exactly that path, a meaningful drop in peak RSS is the
  expected outcome.

  Caveat 1: Julia rarely returns memory to the OS

  Once Julia has grown its heap to N GB, it generally won't shrink back below
  that within a process lifetime, even after objects are freed. So the benefit
  is in the trajectory of a fresh run, not in shrinking an already-running
  process. Each new 96-core run starts with a small heap and the new commits
  should keep it smaller throughout вҖ” but a process that already grew to 95 GB
  won't drop to 60 GB just because allocations slow down.

  Caveat 2: We don't yet know how complete the fix is

  The log analysis identified extraction as the top contributor, but the
  per-call allocation budget after the fixes depends on whether the remaining
  sub-functions still allocate transiently:

  - MeltVolumetrics.calculate_melt_volume (called twice per extraction event)
  - Extractable.update_extractable_meltfrac! /
  Extracted.update_extracted_meltfrac!
  - Volcanism.calculate_number_of_volcanic_markers
  - calculating melt drainage divides (separate +1.6 GB recurring section)

  If any of those still allocate proportional to the partial-melt marker count,
  you'll still see growth вҖ” just slower.

  My estimate

  Pre-fix run: peak ~95 GB at step 1300, still climbing.

  Post-fix run, qualitative outcomes ranked by likelihood:

  1. Most likely: peak RSS settles 20вҖ“40 GB lower (somewhere in 55вҖ“75 GB), with
  growth still decelerating but reaching a flatter asymptote sooner.
  2. Possible: peak comes down only modestly (10вҖ“15 GB) because the residual
  allocators inside the called sub-functions still scale with the workload.
  3. Less likely: no meaningful change, indicating most of the transient peak
  was actually elsewhere (sub-functions, drainage divides, or sparse-matrix
  construction).

  The way to find out cheaply is what we discussed: rerun case4 even partially
  on the 96-core machine, then run the same delta analysis on the new log. If
  extraction's per-call delta drops from ~615 MB to ~50вҖ“100 MB, the fix worked.
  If it's still 300+ MB, the next sub-function needs the same treatment.

  Pair the rerun with --heap-size-hint so you also see whether GC pacing is now
  in the user's control rather than dictated by Julia's allocation heuristics.

вң» Crunched for 42s

вқҜ Since we made these changes could I kill the case4 example on the 96 machine, 
   update the code to the new version , restart from backup and see the effect? 

в—Ҹ Yes, that's actually a better test than starting from scratch вҖ” let me explain
   why, and what to check first.

  Why restart-from-backup is a good test

  - Skips the early-growth phase. The first ~50 timesteps are dominated by JIT
  compilation and first-call buffer allocations (~2.5 GB of fixed cost) that you
   don't care about. Restarting from a step ~800 backup puts you straight into
  the regime where the original log was growing fastest (the steepest growth
  happened steps 700вҖ“1000).
  - Clean Sys.maxrss() baseline. When the Julia process restarts, the OS-level
  resident set resets. The new run's memory trajectory starts fresh at whatever
  the new code uses to load the backup state, then climbs from there. So you can
   directly compare per-extraction-event deltas between old and new code at the 
  same model state.
  - Same partial-melt extent. The thing driving extraction's transient peak in
  the old log was the size of the active partial-melt zone. Restarting from
  backup preserves that zone size, so you're testing the new code at the
  workload regime where the old code was hurting most. Much more diagnostic than
   starting over.

  The one risk: backup compatibility

  The recent commits added new preallocated buffers (compaction_array, the
  marker_indices buffer in MagmaBody, the layered-array buffers, etc.). If any
  of those buffers live as fields inside structs that get serialized to the
  backup, the JLD2 file written by the old code won't have those fields, and
  loading with the new code can fail or load partially.

  Before killing the run, check this:

  git log --oneline -- src/model_data/ src/markers/marker_data/
  src/model_manager/backup/
  git diff f575e92~1..HEAD -- src/model_data/ src/melt_model/ | grep -E "^[+-]"
  | grep -E "::|struct|mutable|field" | head -30

  If the recent commits only added buffers inside transient structs (compute
  scratch, not serialized state), you're fine. If they added fields to anything
  in model.markers.arrays, model.melting.arrays, or anything else that flows
  into MakeBackup.jl, you may need a small migration shim вҖ” usually just
  default-constructing the new fields when loading an old backup.

  BackupManager.jl and LoadBackup.jl in src/model_manager/backup/ are the places
   to look for how the schema is enforced. JLD2 in typereconstruct=true mode is
  forgiving; in strict mode it isn't. Check what the codebase uses.

  Suggested procedure

  1. Confirm a recent backup exists for case4. Check ${output_dir}/backups/ (or
  wherever BackupManager.jl writes them). Note the timestep of the latest one вҖ”
  the closer to the steepest-growth window (steps 700вҖ“1000), the more diagnostic
   the test.
  2. Quick compatibility check (above). Two minutes of grep, saves you a failed
  restart.
  3. Capture the current state вҖ” last few MB : readings from the running log, so
   you have a direct comparison point. Also note the current process RSS via ps
  or top for a sanity check (since maxrss is the high-water mark, current RSS
  may be lower).
  4. Kill cleanly if possible вҖ” let it finish a timestep so the backup at the
  next checkpoint is consistent. (If it's a job scheduler, send SIGTERM not
  SIGKILL.)
  5. Update + restart with --heap-size-hint=80G --gcthreads=24,1 from the same
  backup.
  6. Watch the first ~100 timesteps after restart. That's enough to see whether
  the per-extraction-event delta has dropped meaningfully. If the average jump
  on Finished extracting melt from mantle to magma bodies falls from ~615 MB to
  under ~150 MB, the fixes are working. If it's still 300+ MB, the residual
  allocators (drainage divides, MeltVolumetrics, etc.) need attention next.

  What to expect

  If the commits do what they should, the post-restart trajectory should look
  qualitatively different: smaller per-step jumps, slower (or no) growth in the
  maxrss high-water mark, and Sys.maxrss() settling much closer to the post-load
   resident set. If after 100 timesteps the curve is climbing at the same rate
  as the old log was, that's a clear signal there's still significant transient
  allocation in the path you haven't caught yet.

  Want me to look at the recent commits and tell you whether any of them touched
   backup-serialized state?

вң» Brewed for 55s

вҖ» recap: We're hunting RAM growth in the 96-core EarthBox case4 run; log analysis pinned ~86% of growth to melt extraction, which your recent commits target. Next: verify backup compatibility, then kill, update, and restart from backup to measure the effect. (disable recaps in /config)

вқҜ I am running the restart test now on the 96 core machine with the updated     
  code but without --heap-size-hint and --gcthreads so we can see the efffect   
  of the code changes. I did see the following messages: >> No data found in    
  jld file for layered_partial_melt_indices                                     
  >> No data found in jld file for partial_melt_marker_indices                  
  >> No data found in jld file for marker_spin                                  
  >> No data found in jld file for marker_vy                                    
  >> No data found in jld file for marker_indices_tmp                           
  >> No data found in jld file for pm_layer_offsets                             
  >> No data found in jld file for pm_layer_counts                              
  >> No data found in jld file for marker_vx. Is this OK.                       

в—Ҹ Yes, all of those are safe. Here's why each is fine:

  Categories of the missing fields

  Preallocated scratch buffers added by your recent commits (cc277d3, 49764ed,
  f575e92):
  - layered_partial_melt_indices
  - partial_melt_marker_indices
  - marker_indices_tmp
  - pm_layer_offsets, pm_layer_counts

  These didn't exist when the backup was written. They're filled in fresh each
  call to update_partial_melt_marker_indices! and
  construct_layered_partially_molten_arrays! before any consumer reads them. So
  loading them as zero-default is harmless.

  Per-step output arrays (always overwritten before read each timestep):
  - marker_vx, marker_vy, marker_spin

  These are written by Advection.advect_markers_and_rotate_stress_tensor! (which
   calls the velocity-and-spin update at
  src/advection/velocity_and_spin/VelocityAndSpin.jl:77) every timestep before
  any code reads them. Whatever was in them at backup time doesn't matter; they
  get freshly computed from the Stokes solution.

  What to watch for anyway

  Two things that could bite even though they look safe:

  1. Buffer-size invariants. If any of the new buffers have a code path that
  assumes length(buf) == nmarkers and the default-construction created a
  length-0 vector, you'd get a BoundsError on first use. If the run is past
  pre-solver and into the actual time loop without throwing, you're past that
  risk.
  2. First-timestep pre-solver use. Anything in execute_pre_solver_steps! that
  read marker_vx/vy/spin before the first advection step would see zeros.
  Looking at src/time_loop/TimeLoop.jl:170-197, pre-solver only does
  material/rheology/interpolation work вҖ” nothing reads marker velocities. So
  safe.

  What to check now

  Just confirm the run actually advances past the first timestep вҖ” that's the
  gating signal. If you see a Completed time step N line for the first restart
  timestep without errors, the load went through cleanly and every consumer of
  those fields has populated them as expected.

  The fact that the load process gracefully reports "No data found" rather than
  erroring tells you LoadBackup.jl already has the right schema-evolution
  handling. Good defensive code.

  Now wait for the per-extraction-event deltas in the new log вҖ” that's the
  actual signal you're after.

  likely culprit.

  What I think is happening

  Commit f575e92 added a new field compaction_array::Array3DFloatState to
  model.topography.arrays, preallocated as zeros(Float64, toponum, 20, 9) (see
  src/model_data/topography/arrays/ArrayCollection.jl:69-79).

  The first thing compact_sediment_and_advect_markers! does is assert the
  dimensions (src/compaction/core/MarkerCompaction.jl:65-67):

  @assert size(compaction_array, 1) == length(topo_gridx)
  @assert size(compaction_array, 2) == 20
  @assert size(compaction_array, 3) == 9

  Two ways the backup load could violate this:

  1. The field name isn't in the JLD file at all. The constructor allocates with
   the right size during model construction; LoadBackup.jl overwrites individual
   fields from JLD. If compaction_array simply isn't in the JLD, the
  constructor's correctly-sized buffer should survive вҖ” unless LoadBackup.jl's
  behavior on missing fields is to set them to a default like
  Array3DFloatState(zeros(0,0,0), вҖҰ) rather than leave the existing buffer in
  place. (Note: you didn't see "No data found вҖҰ for compaction_array" in the
  warnings, which is a hint that LoadBackup may have actually loaded something
  under that name.)
  2. There IS a compaction_array entry in the JLD file, but with stale/wrong 
  dimensions вҖ” e.g., a field that existed before f575e92 under the same name
  with a different size, or a placeholder, or it got resized due to a regrid
  that happened after backup save in the original run.

  What I need from you

  Please paste the actual error message that appears immediately above the stack
   trace (one to a few lines, usually starts with ERROR:  and then the type вҖ”
  AssertionError, BoundsError, DimensionMismatch, etc.). That will tell us in
  one line whether it's the assertion I'm guessing or something else entirely.

  Likely quick fix regardless

  If it's an assertion or dimension mismatch on compaction_array, the cleanest
  one-liner fix is to force-reinitialize the buffer after backup load. In
  whatever entry point comes between LoadBackup finishing and run_loop! starting
   (probably in EarthBox.jl after restart), add:

  toponum = length(model.topography.arrays.gridt.array[1, :])
  model.topography.arrays.compaction_array.array = zeros(Float64, toponum, 20,
  9)

  This sidesteps backup-format brittleness entirely for new scratch buffers вҖ”
  they don't need to be in the backup; they should be reconstructed from toponum
   post-load. Same approach would apply to any other new buffers added by recent
   commits if they hit similar errors later.

  Send me the error message and I'll narrow it down further.

  LoadBackup "No data found" warnings only flag the missing scalars/arrays it

  You have two paths. Pick based on whether you want the memory comparison from

  Path A (recommended): patch the new buffers post-load

  Add a single re-initialization step after LoadBackup finishes, before
  run_loop! starts. This rebuilds all the new scratch buffers at their correct
  dimensions, sourced from already-loaded model dimensions.

  The clean place to do this is right after the backup load call in EarthBox.jl
  (around the restart code path вҖ” search for LoadBackup in src/EarthBox.jl to
  find the call site). Add something like:

  function
  reinitialize_new_buffers_after_backup_load!(model::ModelData)::Nothing
      toponum = size(model.topography.arrays.gridt.array, 2)
      marknum = model.markers.parameters.distribution.marknum.value

      # f575e92 вҖ” compaction_array (toponum, 20, 9)
      model.topography.arrays.compaction_array.array =
          zeros(Float64, toponum, 20, 9)

      # ff7dc4e / 49764ed / cc277d3 вҖ” marker/melt scratch buffers
      # (sizes inferred from the constructor calls; copy from the relevant
      # preallocation site to be sure)
      # E.g.:
      # model.melting.arrays.extraction.partial_melt_marker_indices.array = 
  zeros(Int64, marknum)
      # model.melting.arrays.extraction.layered_partial_melt_indices.array = 
  zeros(Int64, marknum)
      # model.melting.arrays.extraction.marker_indices_tmp.array = zeros(Int64, 
  marknum)
      # model.melting.arrays.extraction.pm_layer_offsets.array = zeros(Int64, 
  NLAYERS)
      # model.melting.arrays.extraction.pm_layer_counts.array = zeros(Int64, 
  NLAYERS)

      # Marker velocity/spin (always overwritten before read; just ensure size)
      model.markers.arrays.advection.marker_vx.array = zeros(Float64, marknum)
      model.markers.arrays.advection.marker_vy.array = zeros(Float64, marknum)
      model.markers.arrays.advection.marker_spin.array = zeros(Float64, marknum)

      return nothing
  end

  You'll need to look up the actual paths for the partial-melt buffers (the
  constructor lines in the relevant ArrayCollection.jl files for the melt

  the new code, mirror that allocation here using the same dimension source. I

  The whole thing is one call:

  LoadBackup.load_backup!(...)
  reinitialize_new_buffers_after_backup_load!(model)  # вҶҗ new line
  TimeLoop.run_loop!(model_manager)

  This is a one-time-only utility for handling old backups; once you write a
  fresh backup with the new code, the function becomes a no-op.

  These are all the new state-carrying fields added since 9a9360f (the snapshot
  just before the RAM optimization series). Confirmed by diffing every
  ArrayCollection / group file in model_data/.

  What I confirmed about the load mechanism

  From LoadBackup.jl and SetObjects.jl:

  1. The model is constructed first with the input-file parameters (gives every
  buffer its constructor-correct size).
  2. JLD load iterates over the current model's objects, copies values in place
  into the existing arrays вҖ” and throws a hard error ("1D/2D float/int array
  sizes do not match") if dimensions differ.
  3. If the JLD doesn't contain a key, the constructor's array is preserved
  untouched ("No data found" warning).

  Implication: since your run got past the entire backup load (it didn't throw a
   size-mismatch error), every existing array is already at its correct size,
  and every new buffer is at its constructor-allocated size.

  What this means for the failure

  The failure at sediment-transport line 270 is therefore not a backup-induced
  dimension mismatch on the new buffers вҖ” those would either have been resized
  correctly at construction or you'd already have seen a "sizes do not match"
  error from LoadBackup. Likely candidates instead:

  - An @assert inside the new compaction code that's checking some invariant the
   load satisfied for everything except a specific element of the loaded state
  (e.g., a topography row 7 "extrusive thickness" value, a sealevel state)
  - A BoundsError from a specific algorithmic path that depends on accumulated
  model state (large sediment basin, deep burial markers)
  - A MethodError if some call site passes a new-style buffer where an old-style
   argument was expected

  I genuinely need the actual error text вҖ” the line(s) immediately above [6] 
  run_sediment_transport_model! in the stack trace, which would say ERROR: ... 
  and the exception type and message. Without it, the next step is guessing in
  any of these directions.

  In the meantime вҖ” proper reinit function (in case you want to use it)

  If after seeing the error you still want to reinit the new buffers as
  belt-and-braces, here's the complete, verified version:

  function
  reinitialize_new_buffers_after_backup_load!(model::ModelData)::Nothing
      marknum = model.markers.parameters.distribution.marknum.value
      toponum = size(model.topography.arrays.gridt.array, 2)
      nlayers = 50  # matches Buffers() default

      # AdvectionGroup (commit b973469)
      adv = model.markers.arrays.advection
      if length(adv.marker_vx.array)   != marknum
          adv.marker_vx.array   = zeros(Float64, marknum)
          adv.marker_vy.array   = zeros(Float64, marknum)
          adv.marker_spin.array = zeros(Float64, marknum)
      end

      # BuffersGroup (commits cc277d3, 116ba8a)
      buf = model.melting.arrays.buffers
      if length(buf.partial_melt_marker_indices.array) != marknum
          buf.partial_melt_marker_indices.array  = zeros(Int64, marknum)
          buf.marker_indices_tmp.array           = fill(Int64(-1), marknum)
          buf.layered_partial_melt_indices.array = fill(Int64(-1), marknum)
      end
      if length(buf.pm_layer_counts.array) != nlayers
          buf.pm_layer_counts.array  = zeros(Int, nlayers)
          buf.pm_layer_offsets.array = zeros(Int, nlayers + 1)
      end

      # Topography compaction_array (commit f575e92)
      ca = model.topography.arrays.compaction_array
      if size(ca.array) != (toponum, 20, 9)
          ca.array = zeros(Float64, toponum, 20, 9)
      end

      return nothing
  end

  But please get me the actual error message before applying any fix вҖ” that
  decides whether this function helps at all or whether the bug is somewhere
  else entirely.

в—Ҹ Now I have the smoking gun: size(compaction_array, 1) == length(topo_gridx)
  fails. Both should be toponum but they've diverged. Let me find what resizes
  gridt without resizing compaction_array.

  Searched for 2 patterns, read 1 file (ctrl+o to expand)

в—Ҹ Found the bug вҖ” and it's not a backup-load issue, it's a real bug in the 
  source.

  The error fires because gridt.array and compaction_array.array have
  inconsistent first dimensions. Tracing it:

  src/surface_processes/topography/Topography.jl:252-257:
  function initialize_topo_arrays!(model::ModelData)::Nothing
      toponum = model.topography.parameters.topo_grid.toponum.value
      model.topography.arrays.gridt.array = initialize_topo_array(toponum)
      model.carbonate.arrays.grid_carb.array = initialize_topo_array(toponum)
      return nothing
  end

  This function gets called during Topography.initialize! (after update_toponum!
   recomputes toponum from dx_topo). It reallocates gridt and grid_carb at the
  new toponum вҖ” but commit f575e92 added compaction_array to the topography
  arrays struct without adding a corresponding reallocation here. So:

  1. Arrays(toponum) constructor sizes both gridt (7, T0) and compaction_array
  (T0, 20,В 9), where T0 = input-file toponum.
  2. Topography.initialize! then runs update_toponum!, which computes a new T1
  from dx_topo.
  3. initialize_topo_arrays! reallocates gridt to (7, T1) вҖ” but not 
  compaction_array.
  4. LoadBackup succeeds because the backup's gridt is also at T1 (the toponum
  at backup time matches the current dx_topo-derived value).
  5. First sediment transport call: length(topo_gridx) == size(gridt, 2) == T1,
  but size(compaction_array, 1) == T0. Assertion fires.

  The user's normal (from-scratch) runs probably don't hit this if their input
  parameters happen to give T0 == T1, but the restart workflow exposes the gap.

  The actual fix

  One-line addition at src/surface_processes/topography/Topography.jl:255:

  function initialize_topo_arrays!(model::ModelData)::Nothing
      toponum = model.topography.parameters.topo_grid.toponum.value
      model.topography.arrays.gridt.array = initialize_topo_array(toponum)
      model.carbonate.arrays.grid_carb.array = initialize_topo_array(toponum)
      model.topography.arrays.compaction_array.array = zeros(Float64, toponum,
  20, 9)
      return nothing
  end

  Want me to apply that edit? It's a real source bug, not a workaround вҖ” the
  compaction_array buffer needs the same lifecycle as gridt since they're
  size-coupled.

  One related thing worth checking

  The same bug pattern probably exists for marker-count-sized buffers if there's
   an analogous initialize_marker_arrays! that reallocates marker arrays based
  on a runtime-computed marknum. The new Advection group (3 marker_* arrays) and
   Buffers group (3 marknum-sized arrays) added since the backup era are exposed
   to the same risk. After fixing the immediate compaction_array issue and
  confirming the restart proceeds, I'd recommend grepping for any other
  functions that reallocate model.markers.arrays.*.array = and verifying the new
   groups' buffers are reallocated alongside them. Want me to check that now in
  parallel, so you have the full audit before re-launching?

вқҜ Why does this appear to only happen with restart?                             

в—Ҹ Good question вҖ” the answer hinges on something specific to
  extension-with-moving-boundaries runs. Let me check where the topography grid
  gets resized mid-run.

  Searched for 9 patterns, read 1 file (ctrl+o to expand)

в—Ҹ It would fail on a fresh run too вҖ” restart isn't actually the trigger

  The bug is in the new code's initialization path, not in any restart logic.
  Walking through what happens regardless of restart:

  1. Constructor Topography() at
  model_data/topography/TopographyContainer.jl:32-37 reads
  parameters.topo_grid.toponum.value, which has a hardcoded default of 1001
  (ParameterRegistry.jl:1844-1845).
  2. So Arrays(1001) allocates gridt at (7, 1001) and compaction_array at (1001,
   20, 9).
  3. Later, the user's API call (or model script) invokes
  Topography.initialize!(model; dx_topo=...), which:
    - Computes T_actual = topo_xsize / dx_topo (case4's value вҖ” almost certainly
   not 1001)
    - Sets toponum.value = T_actual
    - Calls initialize_topo_arrays!, which reallocates gridt to (7, T_actual)
  and grid_carb to (7, T_actual)...
    - ... but does NOT touch compaction_array. It stays at (1001, 20, 9).
  4. First sediment_transport call with
  compaction_correction_type="variable_property": length(topo_gridx) == T_actual
   вү  1001 == size(compaction_array, 1). Assertion fires.

  Why you've only seen it on restart

  Three plausible reasons, any of which apply:

  1. You haven't actually tested the new code from scratch on case4 yet. This is
   the most likely answer. You went directly from old-code-running to
  new-code-restart. A fresh from-scratch case4 run with the new code would hit
  the same assertion at the first sediment-transport step that activates
  variable-property compaction.
  2. The benchmarks/tests that did exercise the new path happened to use the 
  default toponum = 1001. Looking at
  src/tests/tests/compaction/marker_compaction/MarkerCompactionTest.jl, the test
   creates compaction_array = make_compaction_array(topo_gridx) from the test's
  own topo_gridx вҖ” so it bypasses the model-level mismatch entirely. The bug is
  only reachable via the production code path that goes through
  model.topography.arrays.compaction_array.
  3. apply_compaction_correction_for_topography_and_markers is only called when 
  compaction_correction_type == "variable_property". If any benchmark used
  "constant_property" (the other branch), the bug wouldn't fire.

  So the bug is "any case that combines (new code) + (variable-property
  compaction) + (input dx_topo giving toponum вү  1001)". Your case4 hits all
  three; the existing tests apparently don't.

  Reframing the fix

  The one-line addition I gave earlier (adding compaction_array.array = 
  zeros(Float64, toponum, 20, 9) to initialize_topo_arrays!) fixes the bug for
  all paths вҖ” restart, fresh, all input combinations. It's the proper fix;
  nothing about it is restart-specific.

  Want me to apply that edit now?

в—Ҹ Edit applied. Now auditing for the same bug pattern with the other new buffers
   вҖ” Advection (3 marker_*-sized arrays) and Buffers (3 marker-sized + 2
  nlayers-sized arrays).

  Searched for 10 patterns, read 1 file (ctrl+o to expand)
в—Ҹ Audit complete вҖ” the compaction_array fix is the only bug of this class

  What I checked

  1. Top-level array reassignments (X.array = ...):
  The only places in the entire src/ that reassign a model-state .array field
  are:
  - Topography.jl:254-256 вҖ” gridt, grid_carb, and now compaction_array (just
  fixed)

  No other code reassigns array fields, so no other "size desync" can develop
  the way the topography one did.

  2. Runtime writes to marknum.value:
  Zero. marknum is computed once from input parameters in
  ModelDataContainer.jl:180-184 and passed by value to
  Markers(marker_parameters), Interpolation(..., marknum), and Melting(marknum).
   Never written to again. So:
  - Advection(marknum) gets the correct marknum at construction.
  - Buffers(marknum) also gets the correct marknum.
  - All marker-sized arrays in the rest of the model are constructed from the
  same value.
  - They stay consistent forever вҖ” there's no analogue of update_toponum! for
  markers.

  3. Runtime writes to xnum.value / ynum.value:
  The only update_input_parameters_with_nums! site (ModelDataContainer.jl:175)
  runs inside the ModelData constructor before any array gets allocated, so
  xnum/ynum are stable from construction onward.

  4. Init-time dimension recompute вҶ’ reallocate pattern:
  The exact pattern that caused the compaction_array bug вҖ” "default constructor
  with placeholder size + later update_X! + initialize_X_arrays! that
  reallocates some arrays from new size" вҖ” exists in only one place: topography.
   The newly-fixed initialize_topo_arrays!. No other group has this lifecycle.

  Why topography is the only one with this hazard

  It's because of how Topography() is constructed in
  ModelDataContainer.jl:186-187:

  _topography = Topography()                               # вҶҗ default 
  toponum=1001
  toponum = _topography.parameters.topo_grid.toponum.value
  ...
  Carbonate(toponum)

  Topography() takes no constructor argument вҖ” it's the only top-level container
   that uses a default placeholder for its primary dimension and re-resizes
  later. Markers, Melting, Interpolation, BoundaryConditions, etc. all take
  their dimensions as constructor arguments derived from the input file, so
  they're correctly sized from the start.

  Recommendation for future-proofing (optional, not blocking)

  Adding new buffers to model.topography.arrays will be a recurring footgun. If
  you add another field to that struct down the road, it'll need to be added to
  initialize_topo_arrays! too or the same bug returns. Two options:

  1. Make Topography() take toponum as an argument like Markers(...) does вҖ” push
   the dimension computation into ModelDataContainer.jl so the Topography
  constructor uses the real toponum from the start. Eliminates
  initialize_topo_arrays! entirely. Larger refactor but cleanest.
  2. Cheaper: add a single @assert size(compaction_array, 1) == 
  size(gridt.array, 2) at the top of Topography.initialize! after
  initialize_topo_arrays! runs, so any future field that gets added without
  being added to the reinit fails immediately and loudly at startup, not deep
  inside a sediment-transport call 1300 timesteps in.

  Neither is required for the current fix to work вҖ” the run should now proceed
  past the assertion. Restart the test and watch the per-extraction-event memory
   deltas.

вң» Brewed for 1m 33s

вҖ» recap: Goal: diagnose RAM growth on the 96-core case4 run and verify recent allocation fixes via restart-from-backup. Just patched the compaction_array reinit bug that blocked restart; next action is to relaunch the restart test and compare per-step memory deltas. (disable recaps in /config)

  Climbing at ~800 MB per active timestep. 7 steps is too short to know if this 
  plateaus. Two possible trajectories:

  1. Plateau early (good): maxrss settles around 70вҖ“80 GB once the transient
  working set has been fully sampled. Would mean the fixes meaningfully reduced
  peak working set vs. the old 95.6 GB.
  2. Climb to similar level: maxrss climbs to ~90+ GB again over the next ~30вҖ“50
   timesteps. Would mean the fixes reduced GC pressure (extraction allocation
  rate) but didn't change peak transient size вҖ” because the peak was actually
  being driven by Stokes-build / pre-solver / fractionation, not extraction.

  What to do next

  - Let it run another 100вҖ“200 timesteps without changing anything. The
  trajectory tells you which scenario you're in.
  - Now is the right time to also add --heap-size-hint. With extraction's
  allocation rate cut, the heap can be capped more aggressively. Try
  --heap-size-hint=70G --gcthreads=24,1 on the next restart. If that holds
  without slowdown, you've meaningfully reduced the resident-memory budget for
  case4.
  - The new top contributors (Stokes build, pre-solver, fractionation, sediment
  transport) are next candidates for buffer-reuse optimization if the trajectory
   ends up climbing back to ~95 GB. The patterns we've already discussed apply:
  clean_non_zero_arrays! slicing, sparse matrix reconstruction, and now the
  sediment-transport / fractionation paths.

  The compaction_array fix is confirmed working вҖ” sediment transport with
  variable-property compaction completed 6 times across this run without errors.

вқҜ OK, given the observations looks for opportunities to reduce RAM pressure by

в—Ҹ RAM optimization plan вҖ” review before applying

  Based on the new log's section-level deltas, organized by expected impact Г·
  effort. Each item follows the same buffer-reuse pattern you've already used
  successfully on melt extraction and compaction_array.

  ---
  Tier 1 вҖ” high impact, drop-in fixes following established patterns

  1.1  Plasticity loop: replace arr.array = copy(...) with copyto!

  File: src/global_plasticity_loop/plasticity_loop/plasticity_loop_nodes/Plastic
  ityLoopNodes.jl:111, 112, 119, 126, 134, 143, 144

  Six grid-sized matrix copies per Picard iteration вҖ” each is a fresh
  Matrix{Float64}(ynum, xnum). Per-step cost = 6 Г— ynum Г— xnum Г— 8 B Г—
  Picard_iters. For your 1151 Г— 306 grid and 5вҖ“10 Picard iters, ~85вҖ“170 MB
  allocated and discarded per timestep just here.

  Change: every line of the form model.X.array = copy(y) вҶ’
  copyto!(model.X.array, y).

  Risk: very low. Pattern-identical to your recent commits.

  1.2  update_nodes_for_plastic_yielding вҖ” preallocate output buffers

  File: src/rheology/plastic_failure/PlasticFailureNodes.jl:72-73

  plastic_yield_new = zeros(ynum, xnum)
  plastic_strain_rate = zeros(ynum, xnum)

  Two fresh grid-sized matrices per Picard iter, passed to caller, immediately
  copied into model.вҖҰetas1.array (#1.1 above), then discarded.

  Change: add plastic_yield_new and plastic_strain_rate as preallocated buffers
  in model.stokes_continuity.arrays.plastic_def (or a new buffers group). Pass
  refs in for in-place fill; remove the redundant copy at the call site.

  Risk: low. Need to ensure callers no longer need to retain the returned arrays
   past the next iteration.

  1.3  Sparse matrix slicing in Stokes/heat builds

  File: src/utils/BuildSysTools.jl:113-116

  return SystemVectors(
      sv.Lii[1:nnz], sv.Ljj[1:nnz],
      sv.Li[1:nnz], sv.Lj[1:nnz], sv.Lv[1:nnz]
  )

  For a Stokes problem nnz is in the tens of millions; this is 5 Г— nnz Г— 8 B вүҲ
  several hundred MB allocated and discarded per Picard iter, then immediately
  handed to sparse(...) which allocates again.

  Change: drop the slicing entirely. Track nnz separately and pass view(sv.Li, 
  1:nnz) etc. directly into sparse(...). sparse accepts AbstractVector, so views
   work.

  Risk: low to medium. Need to verify all callers of clean_non_zero_arrays! use
  the result the same way (some may rely on it being a fresh Vector{Int64} for
  storage; views aren't owned).

  1.4  MUMPS RHS persistent buffer

  Files: src/stokes_continuity_solver/.../SystemSolver.jl:36,
  src/heat_solver/.../SystemSolver.jl:44

  R = copy(model.вҖҰRHS.array)

  A Vector{Float64}(N) per solve where N вүҲ 3В·xnumВ·ynum. ~5 MB on your grid,
  called 6вҖ“10Г—/timestep across Stokes + heat.

  Change: add a rhs_solver_buffer::Vector{Float64} field to
  model.stokes_continuity.arrays.rhs (and equivalent for heat). Use copyto!. Or
  вҖ” verify whether MUMPS actually mutates R; if not, just pass the original
  array.

  Risk: low вҖ” MUMPS docs should confirm whether R is read-only or mutated.

  1.5  Material ID list dictionary cache

  Files: src/markers/marker_materials/utils/MaterialGroupIDs.jl:216 and
  src/markers/marker_materials/get_ids/GetMaterialIDs.jl:226

  get_material_id_lists(model) builds a fresh Dict{String, Vector{Int16}} with
  ~10 internal vectors per call. Called from rheology, melt model,
  serpentinization, surface processes, hydrothermal вҖ” easily 20вҖ“30 calls per
  timestep, each from threaded code paths so allocator contention multiplies on
  96 threads.

  Change: add a material_id_lists::Dict{String, Vector{Int16}} field to
  model.materials.dicts (or wherever materials data lives). Compute once at the
  end of Materials() initialization. Replace every get_material_id_lists(model)
  call site with a direct read of the cached dict. Materials don't change at
  runtime, so no invalidation needed.

  Risk: low. The function is pure with respect to model.materials state, which
  is immutable post-construction.

  ---
  Tier 2 вҖ” medium impact, follow-up after Tier 1

  2.1  Sediment-transport L matrix вҖ” large dense allocation

  File: src/surface_processes/sediment_transport/solver/core/BuildSys.jl:55

  L = zeros(toponum, toponum)
  R = zeros(toponum)

  L is a dense (toponum Г— toponum) matrix. For toponum = 1000, that's 8 MB; for
  5000, 200 MB. Allocated each sediment-transport call.

  Change: preallocate L_buffer and R_buffer in the topography arrays (sized to
  toponum Г— toponum and toponum); use fill!(L_buffer, 0.0) in BuildSys. Resize
  alongside compaction_array in initialize_topo_arrays!.

  Side note: dense (toponum, toponum) looks suspicious вҖ” this is almost
  certainly a tridiagonal or banded system. If so, switching to a sparse or
  banded representation could be a separate scaling win on top of the buffer
  reuse. Worth flagging for the surface-processes maintainer regardless.

  Risk: medium. Need to also add the buffer to initialize_topo_arrays! (the file
   we just edited).

  2.2  Sediment-transport marker displacement buffers

  File:
  src/surface_processes/sediment_transport/solver/core/MarkerAdvection.jl:146, 
  273, 370, 415

  marker_displacement_factors = zeros(marknum)
  marker_displacement = zeros(marknum)

  Four Vector{Float64}(marknum) allocations per call.

  Change: add marker_displacement_factors and marker_displacement as
  preallocated buffers in the existing model.markers.arrays.advection group (or
  a new surface_processes buffers group).

  Risk: low.

  2.3  Marker recycling buffer reuse

  Files: src/boundary_conditions/marker_recycle/utils/ResetMarkers.jl:18-20,
  FractureZoneProperties.jl:24-26, Coordinates.jl:82-83, plus the newx/newy
  zeros in the recycle-type files

  8+ zeros(nrecycle_total) allocations per recycle call, plus fill(Int16(-1), 
  nrecycle_total). The 371 MB single-call delta you saw is partly from this.

  Change: preallocate recycle_buffers in model.bcs.arrays sized to a reasonable
  upper bound (e.g., marknum / 4 or compute from boundary-velocity Г— timestep).
  Slice into views sized to actual nrecycle_total per call.

  Risk: medium вҖ” need to confirm the upper bound is genuinely bounded (extension
   velocity Г— timestep Г— something).

  2.4  BilinearAverage backup copies

  File: src/interpolation/utils/BilinearAverage.jl:240, 246, 261

  copy(stress_change.dsxyn.array)
  copy(stress_change.dsxxn.array)
  copy(model.heat_equation.arrays.temperature.dtkn.array)

  Three grid-sized copies per timestep, in argument lists.

  Change: add 3 persistent _old buffer fields. Use copyto! to populate them
  before the bilinear average call; pass the buffer in.

  Risk: very low.

  2.5  Topography coordinate copies

  File: src/utils/EBCopy.jl:49-53

  function copy_topography_coordinate_arrays(gridt::Matrix{Float64})
      topo_gridx = gridt[1, :]
      topo_gridy = gridt[2, :]
      return topo_gridx, topo_gridy
  end

  gridt[1, :] allocates. Called from sediment transport, lava flow вҖ” 2 Г—
  Vector{Float64}(toponum) per call.

  Change: if callers only need read access, return view(gridt, 1, :), 
  view(gridt, 2, :). If they need mutable copies, keep the function but provide
  an in-place variant copy_topography_coordinate_arrays!(buf_x, buf_y, gridt)
  for hot paths.

  Risk: medium вҖ” need to audit callers to confirm whether they mutate the
  returned arrays.

  ---
  Tier 3 вҖ” investigation before changes

  3.1  Profile gabbroic fractionation

  You haven't yet identified where the 679 MB/call delta comes from. The file
  src/melt_model/fractionation/Fractionation.jl only shows vcat allocations,
  which would be small. The cost is presumably downstream вҖ” likely
  get_material_id_lists() calls (covered by Tier 1.5) plus possibly something
  inside Threads.@threads loops. Recommend @btime or --track-allocation=user on
  a few timesteps before deciding.

  3.2  Type-stability audit on the pre-solver chain

  The pre-solver wraps ~16 sequential marker-loop functions. If any of them have
   type-unstable code (abstract field types, untyped globals, anonymous closures
   capturing variables) the allocations per marker Г— marknum Г— threads add up
  fast. Run @code_warntype on the top call sites in RockProperties, Rheology,
  MeltModel.update_marker_*. Anywhere you see ::Any or ::Union{...} with many
  alternatives, that's a candidate. This is search-not-fix; output is a list of
  functions to consider stabilizing.

  3.3  Check whether marker_recycling and melt_solidification actually fire 
  every timestep

  The 371 MB and 639 MB single-call entries in the new log might be one-time
  setup, not per-step. If they only fire occasionally, they don't drive the
  recurring growth and Tier 2.3 might be lower priority than I've ranked it.
  Easy to check with grep -c "Finished marker recycling" case4p2.out.

  ---
  Operational (no code changes)

  O.1  Add --heap-size-hint and --gcthreads to the next 96-core run

  With extraction's allocation rate cut, GC has more headroom to keep up.
  Suggested:

  julia --project=. --threads=96 --gcthreads=24,1 --heap-size-hint=70G ...

  Pick the heap-size-hint value based on the new log's plateau once it's run
  further. Right now it's at 66.6 GB after 7 steps and still climbing; somewhere
   between 70 GB and 85 GB is plausible target. Bisect.

  O.2  Per-timestep GC.gc() diagnostic

  Temporarily add GC.gc() at the bottom of the per-timestep loop in
  src/time_loop/TimeLoop.jl:163 (inside the timestep for loop). Run for ~50
  timesteps. If maxrss stops climbing, the residual growth is GC-pacing and Tier
   1 + heap-size-hint will largely solve it. If maxrss still climbs, there's
  still allocate-and-leak behavior somewhere we haven't found. Diagnostic only вҖ”
   remove after the test.

  ---
  Recommended order

  1. Run another 100вҖ“200 timesteps of case4 first, with the existing fix and
  --heap-size-hint=80G. Establish whether it plateaus and where. That data
  should drive prioritization.
  2. Tier 1.5 (material ID dict cache) вҖ” biggest low-effort win, affects many
  call sites simultaneously, no risk of behavioral changes.
  3. Tier 1.1 + 1.2 together (plasticity loop) вҖ” same code path, same kind of
  edit.
  4. Tier 1.3 (sparse matrix slicing) вҖ” biggest single allocation hotspot, but
  needs careful review of callers.
  5. Tier 1.4 (MUMPS RHS) вҖ” quick win.
  6. Tier 3 investigations to scope Tier 2 better.
  7. Tier 2 items as needed depending on what the extended run shows.

  Ready for your review. Tell me which items you want me to draft patches for
  first.

  171 active timesteps; +36 GB above load baseline. Compared to the pre-fix
  run's +45 GB above its baseline, the new transient working set is roughly 20%
  smaller вҖ” modest but real. Run is still climbing intermittently; not
  converged.

  Two distinct things happened

  1. A MUMPS memory failure around step 1342

  Errors at lines 82594-82624:
  ** ERROR RETURN ** FROM DMUMPS INFO(1)=-20  INFO(2)=7937704   вҶҗ out of memory
  in factorization
  ** ERROR RETURN ** FROM DMUMPS INFO(1)=-3                       вҶҗ solve phase
  failed (factorization hadn't finished)

  INFO(1)=-20 means MUMPS's internal workspace was too small to hold the 
  factorization вҖ” not Julia heap, not system RAM. MUMPS pre-allocates workspace
  based on its analysis phase plus the memory_relax_perc margin (currently 25%).
   When the actual factorization needed more memory than predicted, it failed.

  Your code's retry loop ("Try again with the same parameters") then re-ran. The
   retry must have eventually succeeded since the run continued to step 1387 вҖ”
  but the retries are likely what caused the +10 GB Julia-side jump from 88.7 GB
   to 98.1 GB between steps 1335 and 1375.

  This is a separate problem from the optimization plan and likely needs
  memory_relax_perc bumped to 35% or 50% in your case4 input.

  2. Confirmed and revised optimization targets

  Section-level delta attribution for this 171-step window:

  confirming the buffer-reuse fixes work.

  both at ~480 MB per call.

  Updated optimization plan priorities

  This data means the original plan's ordering should change:

  Highest impact based on real measurements:
  - Pre-solver chain (Tier 1.5 in original plan: material ID list dictionary 
  cache) stays Tier 1, since it's called from across the pre-solver chain ~20вҖ“30
   times per timestep.
  - Gabbroic fractionation is the surprise dominant grower вҖ” needs
  investigation. Its top-level allocation only shows vcat for material IDs, so
  the cost is downstream. Top suspects: per-thread allocations inside its
  Threads.@threads loop at Fractionation.jl:110, plus get_material_id_lists()
  calls, plus a bisection-method change (329220d) that may be allocating per
  marker. This wasn't on the original plan and probably should be TierВ 1.
  - Picard loop transient peak of 6 GB likely tied to the MUMPS failure event,
  but if the same call is averaging 1 GB per timestep that's still significant.
  Combine the original Tier 1.1, 1.2, 1.3, 1.4 вҖ” they all live in this section.

  Lower priority than originally:
  - Melt extraction sub-functions are already deeply optimized; further work
  there is unlikely to pay off.

  New addition to the plan:
  - Bump MUMPS memory_relax_perc from 25% to 50% in the case4 input. This is a
  one-line change in your case input, no code modification. Should eliminate the
   factorization OOM at step ~1342 and the resulting Julia-side memory ratchet.

  Recommendation

  Two parallel paths:

  1. Operational fix for the MUMPS issue: change memory_relax_perc=50 (orВ 75) in
   case4. This isn't a code modification вҖ” it's a case parameter. Should let the
   run get further without triggering the workspace recovery path. Worth doing
  on the next restart regardless.
  2. Code optimization priorities вҖ” in this order:
    - Investigate gabbroic fractionation's per-call 477 MB cost (Tier 3.1 from
  original plan, now elevated to highest priority because it's a top-2
  contributor). The two vcat calls don't explain 477 MB; the cost must be in
  Threads.@threads loops, downstream get_material_id_lists(), or the

  Revised interpretation

  If MUMPS runs in an external process and the retry path doesn't touch the main
   Julia process's RAM, then:

  - The +10 GB climb between steps 1335 (88.7 GB) and 1375 (98.1 GB) was

  - It's the same dynamic as the earlier climbs: occasional transient peaks
  pushing the maxrss high-water mark up over time, driven by the sections in the
   per-section delta table.
  - The MUMPS memory_relax_perc recommendation can be dropped from the
  optimization plan вҖ” it's a stability concern in its own right (failed
  factorizations waste compute), but it's not a memory-usage issue from the main
   Julia process's perspective.

  Implication for the climb pattern

  The Julia-side trajectory still shows a non-converged climb. Three
  observations:

  1. The climb is gradual and intermittent: long flat periods (e.g., steps
  1275вҖ“1295) interrupted by climb periods (e.g., 1355вҖ“1375). Consistent with
  rare large-transient events from one of the top sections setting new highs.
  2. Pre-solver and fractionation deliver consistent ~480 MB/call (avg) but
  their max values are 1.8 GB. Those 1.8 GB outliers are likely what bumped the
  high-water mark during the climb periods вҖ” most calls stay below the previous
  peak, occasional ones don't.
  3. Picard loop's 6.0 GB max (vs 1.0 GB avg) is still suspicious independent of
   MUMPS. The 6Г— spike on a single Picard call is the kind of outlier that
  pushes maxrss up in one shot. If this isn't MUMPS-driven, it's worth
  understanding what made one Picard iteration that much heavier than the others
   вҖ” possibly when the Stokes pattern grew (more nonzeros in the sparse system)
  or when plasticity yielded across more nodes.
